0439023513|2.0|**THIS REVIEW CONTAINS SPOILERS**  I came into reading Mockingjay with the knowledge that a lot of people found it disappointing, but I decided to read it anyway to see for myself, to see how the story ends and also because this trilogy has been a quick read. The beginning of the book involves a lot of reminders of what happened in previous books, which was kind of tedious to get through, but then once the story starts moving in this one I actually liked it. There is a decent amount of character development and descriptions of what is going on, where it's not just about everyone trying not to get killed. The depiction of various characters' mental breakdowns is well done, and I enjoyed reading about the preparations for going into the final battle.  But Mockingjay turns sour in Part III, the last third of the book. What had previously been a slower paced, fairly interesting story suddenly turns into a funhouse of horrors, where all these characters we'd finally been getting to know, along with a bunch of new characters we didn't know at all, die in all sorts of bizarre ways to the point where it's ridiculous. And if you thought Catching Fire was kind of cheap in how every chapter ended in a sudden cliffhanger and it seemed like the author was mostly just having fun thinking of novel ways to kill people, this one is worse. The deaths pile up quickly and get weirder and weirder until we get the giant horde of mutant lizards that go around decapitating everyone in sight. And then the cage of children gets blown up, but not before Katniss's sister happens to wander in there and she dies in a scene that had me going, \\"Wait, what?\\" and being so confused about this arbitrary sequence of events that I wasn't even sad about it.  The ending with Katniss's mother and Gale is disappointing in how it's basically non-existent. I had a hard time believing those two would just leave Katniss behind after everything, but then Katniss ends up with Peeta by default over the course of two pages that are so bland and generically written that they're completely devoid of emotion. I was glad that Katniss didn't end up in a position of power, because she really is just a kid, and by the end she's a permanently emotionally damaged kid. But the ending felt rushed and unsatisfying.

B00000293E|5.0|Willie Dixon was the house songwriter, producer, sometimes standup bass player, and sometimes vocalist for The Chess Brothers studio in Chicago.  His songs formed the basis for the blues from the late 40s onwards, and carried over into rock.  Most of his songs were perfomed by others, such as Muddy Waters[[ASIN:B000002Q40 The Chess Box :Muddy Waters]], and a good collection by various performing artists is found in [[ASIN:B000002P8I Chess Box]] along with his bio.  The present collection is 9 of Willie Dixon's songs performed by himself.  It is a somewhat slim representation of his work.<br /><br />Willie Dixon was an advocate for other musicians, getting their work properly credited and produced.  He served as a springboard for many.  Honors came late, often after his death.  Vicksburg, the city of his birth, has a mural of Willie playing the standup bass among the murals on the Vicksburg floodwall, a local tourist attraction, and also named a street after him.  Like many blues musicians, he left Mississippi and moved north to Chicago.  The booklet included in the jewel case with the present recording provides scant information.  A more complete booklet is included with the aforementioned Chess<br />Box collection (which has always been a best buy).

1608193381|1.0|I generally try to finish books as sometimes books can improve as you get into them. This book was just painful, though. I suspected something was wrong on the very first page when the author, talking about the ongoing financial catastrophe, says (p. xiii), \\"This catastrophe has ultimately been created by the free-market ideology that has ruled the world since the 1980s,\\" only to proceed to talk about how \\"there is really no such thing as a free market\\" (p. xvi). Thus he blames the ongoing financial calamity on something that he himself says doesn't exist. Some of what he says serves as a good description of economic and political realities, but much of it undermines the very argument he's trying to make - that the free market doesn't work. Would a truly free market be as good for the world as its most ardent proponents claim? I don't know because we've never really had one and likely never will.<br /><br />To pose just one question to the author (or anyone else who'd like to blame the current situation on the free market): How can the market be free when the Federal Reserve sets interest rates? Monetary policy (i.e., tinkering with the money supply by the Fed) introduces many distortions into the system that over the long term create massive imbalances. In his great book [[ASIN:0471732834 A History of Interest Rates, Fourth Edition (Wiley Finance)]], Sidney Homer shows that over very long periods of time (like practically all of recorded history, about 5,000 years), interest rates fluctuated in a range of 6 to 12%. When the Fed keeps interest rates at 1% for years and years, that's not a free market. The free market would not allow such a thing to happen. In addition, the free market didn't decide to bail out the huge banks that took massive risks that eventually blew up. The Fed and Treasury did that, creating the moral hazard that will lead to the next crisis. No free market advocate I am aware of supported bailing out the big banks or any of the other interventions the Fed and federal government have been undertaking for the last several decades.<br /><br />At any rate, this book is very short on cogent argumentation as to why the free market doesn't work and long on non sequiturs, flimsy evidence, and contradictions. Personally, I actively seek out books or other materials that argue carefully against a position I've read elsewhere. I'm an agnostic on most things, including on whether the free market is all that. But I'm open to believing that it's as good as it gets. So far, I've read little to convince me that there's a better alternative, and this book is one of the worst I've read recently. Stay away.ons the Fed and federal government have been undertaking for the last several decades.<br /><br />At any rate, this book is very short on cogent argumentation as to why the free market doesn't work and long on non sequiturs, flimsy evidence, and contradictions. I have never been brand loyal to either Intel or AMD as each has had it's generations of dominance.  Unfortunately for AMD, not only is Intel's current line of I-Core chips remarkably better performers, this is the first CPU I have ever felt was actually worse than the previous generation.<br /><br />My experience with the FX 8120 was very negative.  When I first started up the machine I noticed a tendency for the windows install to go through spurts of activity and then stalling.  I chalked it up to hardware recognition issues as I was installing XP 64.  Once I got the OS on, the first thing I did was install drivers and update the motherboards bios.  I figured this would take care of any external performance issues, allowing the chip itself to finally run full speed.<br /><br />The stuttering continued.  I had just upgraded the system from AMD 8A APU processor as this machine was intended to be an HTPC.  I found I wanted to run some programs that required some higher horsepower however and decided to move to a larger case with a dedicate video card and full size AT power supply.  The first thing I noticed was that on nearly every front, the machine ran worse.  I spent an entire afternoon making sure every driver was updated, the bios's updated, and all the bios settings optimized.  I never was able to make the chip run even remotely well, and I noticed around this time that just idling the CPU was running nearly 65 degrees Celsius (normally I would expect 48-50 degrees Celsius).<br /><br />I returned the CPU and opted to go with a core I-5 2600k which ran about $20 more. B004XE0P5E|4.0|Three years on from his startling debut [[ASIN:B0011HF6GE For Emma, Forever Ago]] and after some brilliant collaborations with the likes of Kanye West ([[ASIN:B004BSIJ9Q My Beautiful Dark Twisted Fantasy [Explicit]]]), Justin Vernon's solo vehicle Bon Iver is very much 'of the moment'. Ditching the subtle intimacy of his debut, [[ASIN:B004XE0P5E Bon Iver]] is a sonic expansion; adding horns, strings and a more lush sense of production to create a fuller and ultimately more grandiose album. The influences of his 'mainstream' collaborations are evident here and results in a relatively accessible sound. Songs such as [[ASIN:B005C7MWLQ Holocene]] and [[ASIN:B005C7N0SA Calgary]] show the way that Vernon has managed to adapt his folk leanings into a more fleshed out sound. Whereas on his debut the instrumentation and production seemed peripheral to the lyrics and his frail falsetto, here all of the elements manage to work beautifully in unison. There truly is some innovative and beautiful songwriting to be found on this album. The lyrics are still as hard to decipher not only in their literal form but also when sung by Vernon. Of minor concern is the rather dated feel of some of the songs. This is especially true of the album's closing track [[ASIN:B005C7N1N4 Beth/Rest]] while beautiful, is remarkably dated with it's Bonnie Raitt nuances ([[ASIN:B000T1FR8A I Can't Make You Love Me]]). Alongside this, the album falls victim to a 'mid-length slump'. The tracks [[ASIN:B0054JUTIA Michicant]],[[ASIN:B005C7MZ1S Hinnom, TX]] and [[ASIN:B0054JUTYE Wash.]] are rather lackluster affairs that simply feel like a retread of preceding songs. Bon Iver at times suffers from a lack of variety and an upbeat tempo song would do wonders to break up the mid-tempo monotony. However, it is impossible to deny Vernon's exquisite songwriting capabilities and the aching beauty found within the music.  8/10.

B001FB55H6|2.0|I was unfamiliar with \\"The Watchmen\\" graphic novel, and had wanted to see the movie when it was in theaters.  I saw this on my home theater via Amazon's streaming video.  First, the good: The story line has some interesting components - an homage to comic book characters of the past, some interesting twists in the relationships of the characters, and the compelling \\"back story\\" of some of the super heroes - especially Rorschach.  I can see how it would work well as a graphic novel.  There were very good performances by Jeffrey Dean Morgan as \\"The Comedian\\", Stephen McHattie as Hollis Mason, Carla Gugino as \\"Silk Spectre\\", and especially Jackie Earle Haley as \\"Rorschach\\".  The art direction of the movie is very good.  The visuals of the dark world of the super heroes are excellent, and the vision of Mars was beautiful.  The main problem is, in the end, these are comic book heroes.  There's just not enough story to support a three hour run time.  At some point, even the best fight sequences become tiresome if it's the sixth or seventh time a fight breaks out on screen.  A second problem is the uneven quality of the acting.  After that, the acting is sometimes there, sometimes not, and when it's not, you start looking at your watch, wishing the scene would end already.  For me, the biggest problem is the run time.  If the movie was around 100 minutes, I probably would have rated it very highly; at two hours, pretty good, but at three hours it's not a good return on the time investment.  I recognize that a director's cut is likely to be a bit self-indulgent, but 187 minutes is a lot to ask from someone when most of it rises to little more than a darker and technically more sophisticated version of the Batman TV series from the 1960's.

B0072GPQ72|5.0|I thoroughly enjoyed this film. After seeing it at the theater I came onto Amazon, as I often do, to add it to my wishlist. I then read the unexcited reviews that were found on the product page and thought I should write a review to posit a counter opinion. The other reviews posted didn't seem to have enjoyed the film, two major criticisms being that they found it melodramatic and heavy handed, comments like these make me wonder if audiences have become so cynical that they view any attempt at displaying open/honest emotion to be an attempt at manipulation. Everyone has differing opinions but I feel this film will appeal to anyone who enjoys stylistically well made films and/or anyone who enjoys stories about bonds/will being tested. In a general sense Spielberg uses the story of this horse to explore the human condition and explore some very well done set pieces/environments.<br /><br />As I saw it, the horse is a metaphor for hope and how it can be shared and spread, how it can inspire and endure. I don't mean this in an over sentimental way but just in a very real way. We are all hopeful for different things, big and small, and this film is about how under the right conditions and with the right persistence that hope can avoid being snuffed out or lost.<br /><br />The film, based on a play I have not seen and cannot compare it to, is episodic as the horse goes from owner to owner during the years of World War 1. The fact that the film was episodic didn't make it feel chopped up or give it the feel of a broken narrative. I felt the through line of the film was the human condition and the traveling horse facilitated the telling of several viewpoints while exploring the excellent recreation of a time period. The acting was top notch by everyone seen on screen. To counter another criticism, the main human character, a boy named Albert, does show strong affection for the horse, a character in the film quips \\"come on now boy, it's not as if he were a dog\\". I didn't find this relationship to be strange, the horse simply becomes very important to Albert because as we see he doesn't have much to his life and the horse is something he gets that then works to make his life better. Perhaps I also never found it strange as I always found the horse to be representative of hope rather than as just some random animal.<br /><br />The writing in terms of dialogue flowed and felt incredibly natural, as it does in most Spielberg films. John Williams score, while clearly a John Williams score, is the perfect mix of innocence, action/adventure and drama as the story calls for it. I would strongly compare the pacing and acting to Catch Me if You Can, as that film also had several major settings that shifted as the film progressed. I found War Horse to actually be better paced as it has a few more settings (about 5, possibly 6 in total?) and each one is shorter than the ones in the aforementioned film. Best of all the film actually ends when it ends, the story concludes and there are no tacked on or forced endings for the sake of pandering or over explaining as many recent films have had.<br /><br />As far as the directing, I don't know if one can compare anything to the seeming effortless magic he created in his early films or certain efforts from the 90's but this would be Spielberg above his recent best and closer to those older films in quality. Everything unfolds visually in such an easy to follow an familiar way while still being unique and involving. There are some masterful shots that recall the opening of Saving Private Ryan but in an artistically different way. This is unquestionably due in part to the fact that this film has been made family friendly, while that could serve as a criticism I didn't find the film to be aimed specifically at families, though it has been made to facilitate viewing by younger children. Rather than illustrating the pure violence of battle as he did in SPR, Spielberg spends his time in this film showing the brutality of war in a creative fashion that suggests the horror more than displaying it. There is one such scene in particular where mounted soldiers ride into battle and on the other end of the shot their empty horses emerge without many of the riders they once carried.<br /><br />In a sea of films about special effects, high concept stories or big names here is a film that is a complete film on its own without any gimmicks, as fun as gimmicks can be (Mi4 for example). This film is an actual experience for those who are open to it. Girl with the Dragon Tattoo was an excellently crafted film that pushed the dark and grittiness, War Horse is a excellently crafted film that pushes the lighter elements but both are equally enjoyable and excellently made. Watching a good Spielberg film is like watching the epitome of what film is. It's like more recently watching Christopher Nolan's films, there are always flaws in any film but films such as theirs are so carefully and purposefully made as films that they are engaging and fully engrossing.<br /><br />One potential weakness I will admit, though I feel a reviewing of the film would diminish it, is that the film doesn't have as strong an arc as most films. The characters change but so much of it is internal here. The horse's first owner does change but he is absent through the middle of the film, the various other owners also change in varying ways but all during their own vignettes. The horse itself has changed in much the same way as his owner as suggested by the final shot of the film, but perhaps critical audience members won't pick up on this. That said, one could (and I would say should) view this subtlety as purposeful. The film isn't as much about how the boy or the horse has changed but how despite all they've been through they managed to stay so much the same, they maintain what could easily have been lost.<br /><br />I dunno, I personally love it (5 Stars) but for objectivity I'll give it 4/5 for general audiences, I think most everyone I described at the beginning of this review will like this film if they view it. For those who feel it is heavy handed and melodramatic go and watch Girl with the Dragon Tattoo, its also an excellent film but there is no overt display of emotion to be found there. Thanks for your timeutality of war in a creative fashion that suggests the horror more than displaying it. There is one such scene in particular where mounted soldiers ride into battle and on the other end of the shot their empty horses emerge without many of the riders they once carried.<br /><br />In a sea of films about special effects, high concept stories or big names here is a film that is a complete film on its own without any gimmicks, as fun as gimmicks can be (Mi4 for example). This film is an actual experience for those who are open to it. Girl with the Dragon Tattoo was an excellently crafted film that pushed the dark and grittiness, War Horse is a excellently crafted film that pushes the lighter elements but both are equally enjoyable and excellently made. Watching a good Spielberg film is like watching the epitome of what film is. It's like more recently watching Christopher Nolan's films, there are always flaws in any film but films such as theirs are so carefully and purposefully made as films that they are engaging and fully engrossing.<br /><br />One potential weakness I will admit, though I feel a reviewing of the film would diminish it, is that the film doesn't have as strong an arc as most films. The characters change but so much of it is internal here. The horse's first owner does change but he is absent through the middle of the film, the various other owners also change in varying ways but all during their own vignettes. The horse itself has changed in much the same way as his owner as suggested by the final shot of the film, but perhaps critical audience members won't pick up on this. That said, one could (and I would say should) view this subtlety as purposeful. The film isn't as much about how the boy or the horse has changed but how despite all they've been through they managed to stay so much the same, they maintain what could easily have been lost.<br /><br />I dunno, I personally love it (5 Stars) but for objectivity I'll give it 4/5 for general audiences, I think most everyone I described at the beginning of this review will like this film if they view it. For those who feel it is heavy handed and melodramatic go and watch Girl with the Dragon Tattoo, its also an excellent film but there is no overt display of emotion to be found there. B005LAIGPA|4.0|It's always been a dream of teenage boys to have telekinesis. Be it just trying to get the door close when you're trying to sleep or shoving the idiot who's driving behind you off the road, telekinesis is the ultimate form of passive/aggression.  Although the latest movie trend has been to depict realistic superheroes, few have truly committed to the format.  Chronicle is so steeped in its high school subculture that the word superhero is never even mentioned.<br /><br />Chronicle starts with high school and builds its plot from there: disaffected loner Andrew Detmer (Dane DeHaan), his more well-adjusted cousin Matt Garetty (Alex Russell), and popular kid Steve Montgomery (Michael B. Jordan) discover alien crystals.  The crystals vibrate and hum, inflicting nosebleeds on the three teenagers, all of it recorded by Andrew on film.  The camera winks out only to resume weeks later with a demonstration of their newfound powers.<br /><br />Chronicle's name is obscure but apt because Andrew films everything.  The entire movie is pieced together from Andrew's camera and -- later -- security cameras, news footage, and home video of the inevitable conclusion.<br /><br />Chronicle nails what kids with superpowers would do with them: They'd play pranks, of course.  Their powers are not limited to bending spoons however. Their telekinesis escalates from nudging things to shoving things to creating personal shields and eventually flight.  When that happens, all bets are off.<br /><br />In the beginning the triumvirate, bound by their collective secret, tries hard to support each other. Drunk fathers, social misadventures with girls, and high school popularity all gnaw away at their common bond. Also, a human being flying thousands of feet in the air is dangerous.<br /><br />Chronicle records all of this with the mundanity of a reality show, but it rises above the Blair Witch Project genre when it takes to the skies, where the wind roars constantly.  Some of the special effects are less spectacular - the ones that involve peculiar lighting, like a juggling talent show, are obviously CGI - but when it counts Chronicle's special effects are superb.<br /><br />Chronicle pulls no punches in portraying a broken home life and how a superpower only magnifies the pain.  The inevitability of its protagonists' fates is also the film's biggest flaw. It's no surprise who will take on the role of the hero and who will be the villain.<br /><br />But that doesn't detract from the breathtaking conclusion, a terrifying and (due to the multiple camera POV) disorienting display of telekinesis powered by human emotion.  It's worth the price of admission alone.<br /><br />It's easy to chalk Chronicle up as an unrealistic fantasy.  But I couldn't get it out of my head that if you replace the word \\"telekinesis\\" with \\"gun\\"...the body count at the end of the film doesn't seem so unrealistic after all.r lighting, like a juggling talent show, are obviously CGI - but when it counts Chronicle's special effects are superb. 0307957934|3.0|Robert Harris's latest novel, \\"The Fear Index,\\" rests comfortably in Geneva, Switzerland, a town where the rich go to get richer.  In a brisk novel covering approximately 24 hours, Harris attempts to weave together a complex tale of high finance, technology, corruption, and metaphysics.  This spicy concoction has some good moments but ultimately fails to reach the heights of his other books (\\"Imperium,\\" \\"Fatherland\\").  Alex Hoffman, American hedge fund manager and billionaire, contemplates a mysterious package in his inner sanctum - a palace/estate far too vast for just him and his wife.  Somebody has anonymously sent him a first-edition of Darwin worth more than my first and second cars put together.  This intriguing development almost gets forgotten in the ensuing chaos as a madman breaks into his house and, by the way, on the next day he's scheduled to meet with billionaire investors to role out his new hedge fund . . . a fund that will be managed solely by computers executing Hoffman's revolutionary new algorithm.  This algorithm teaches the computers how to invest based on fear.  Fear is something that Hoffman is going to grow all too familiar with over the next day, starting with the realization that the person who sent Hoffman the Darwin book appears to be . . . Hoffman himself.  Ultimately \\"The Fear Index\\" is about the corrupting influence of money and how our modern economy appears to be designed to eliminate humans.  Harris must envision a world where one person will inevitably program a computer so smart and so powerful that the rest of us will become meaningless.  Harris asks, if a smart computer can make investments based on fear, and we live in a completely wired world, how soon will it take the computer to figure out how to generate its own fear?  \\"The Fear Index\\" may be timely, but ultimately it's not that thrilling a thriller.  Hoffman is a poor protagonist, a billionaire engineer who also happens to be a colossal jerk.  If Hoffman were Falstaffianin proportions then \\"The Fear Index\\" would still be interesting (perhaps spectacularly so), but Hoffman is such a robot that he never engenders any sympathy.  Even his wife gets disgusted with him when he admits that he's worth about a billion dollars . . . does he have any priorities outside of making money?  Harris appears on several pages to be setting us up for a high finance version of \\"2001:  A Space Odyssey,\\" with Hoffman's computer serving as HAL, but we never get quite that far.  Too bad - another opportunity missed.  If you've got a long plane flight, you could do a lot worse than \\"The Fear Index,\\" but you could do a lot better, too.

B0057D876Y|5.0|How appropriate that vinyl is making a music industry comeback, and that ABBA fans can celebrate \\"The Vistors\\" right on time for it's 30th anniversary. This would be the first full length studio album ABBA would record after both couples had divorced, and it would also be ABBA'S last album ever. The years leading up to this had held much change for their personal lives, and this was certainly reflected by the more mature, progressive atmosphere in their music. While a few songs seemed to wink at the happy ABBA of the past (\\"Head Over Heals\\" and \\"Two For The Price Of One\\"), songs like \\"Soldiers\\", \\"I Let The Music Speak\\", and the title track clearly displayed that ABBA were taking their listeners to a new, more sophisticated place. And If we thought the lyrics were personal on \\"The Winner Takes It All\\", who dreamed we would be looking even deeper within via songs like \\"One Of Us\\", and \\"When All Is Said and Done\\", speaking to the pain, regret, and even forgiveness of love gone wrong.  \\"Slipping Through My Fingers\\" deals with the present day heartache a parent feels watching a child growing up,and regretting lost opportunities. This song's less harmony layered moment as Frida joins Agnetha at the beginning of verse two might be one of the closest things fans can get to \\"ABBA unplugged\\". This song ends with a clock ticking, that takes us to the last track, \\"Like An Angel Passing Through My Room\\", an ABBA lullaby that leaves fans wanting more of a style that pre-dated Enya. There is something about Frida's performances on this album that fans have consistently taken note of. It is credit to Bjorn and Benny's production values to be sure. Frida would have one more ABBA song to demonstrate her beautiful, soaring lead vocal stylings on a track called \\"Cassandra\\". This was recorded during ABBA's final sessions in 1982.  And while Frida would record her \\"Somethings Going On\\" solo album shortly after \\"The Visitors\\" release, it is the ABBA recordings directly before and after her solo effort that truly showcase her vocal talent. The ABBA singles collection that followed in 1982, bearing the title \\"The First 10 Years\\" certainly gave hope that more ABBA albums would follow. One can only imagine where the next sessions would have taken them.

1619492881|3.0|Howards End is a look at the philosophical and socioeconomic state of England prior to World War I.  It is essentially told from three points of view: 1). The Schlegel family, wealthy and progressive with socialist ideals, 2). The Wilcox family , wealthy, \\"old school\\" traditionalists, and 3). Leonard Bast, a lower middle class young man seeking to better himself both culturally and socially.  Slowly their stories (both past and present) become intertwined and their varying opinions are thrown into stark relief... sometimes in surprising ways.  All the while, periodic philosophical discussions take place about wealth, society, gender equality, death.  Meh.<br /><br />While it makes for an interesting study of the society and ideals of the time, the essential lack of plot in Howards End left me yawning my way through much it.  There wasn't even enough witty dialogue to make up for the lack of action. On Beauty at least had some killer dialogue.  As was the case with On Beauty, there is a huge cast of characters, some of whom are well-developed and many of whom barely exist beyond their names and basic relationship to the others. Margaret and Helen, whom the story tends center around,  are the only fully developed characters.  The others came off as flat and one dimensional.  While Howards End made for an interesting period study, its limited characterization and even more limited plot still made it an essentially unsatisfying read.<br /><br />The Bottom Line: I know it's a classic, but Howards End did very little for me.  Mildly interesting at best.<br /><br />This discussion originally appeared on my blog.  See my profile for details.

B002BU0DPE|3.0|I've been using the PX21s daily for a little over a year now.  While I really like them and enjoy the sound when playing games, they aren't as good as they could be.  If you are playing with a bluetooth phone headset, do everyone a favor and at least upgrade to these.  They ARE worth the money but be gentle with them.  Here are my only real complaints about the headset.  1.  Sound is sometimes confusing.  You will hear someone near you but its difficult to tell where they are.  2.  The ear cups are designed to swivel.  Its a great feature, however, its poorly executed.  Both of my swivels broke.  My sister's broke and so did my coworkers.  This seems to be an incredibly common problem and after taking the headsets apart, I see why.  The plastic for the that part of the headset gets so thin, that it can't stand any perpendicular pressure.  Turtle Beach could remedy this by substituting just that area with a small piece of aluminum or zinc rather than plastic.  It would make the headset a couple of dollars more expensive but it would be worth it. I've been able to reassemble both sides of my headset by using some quick setting Loctite plastic epoxy.  I use the epoxy around the joint and so it doesn't swivel anymore but they still function fine without that capability.  If you do buy these, be very cautions with those joints and buy some plastic epoxy just in case.

B003V4AK8E|4.0|I have never been fond of the Dualshock controller, so I was excited to see this Xbox-style controller for the PS3.  The Pro Elite Wireless Controller is by no means a replica Xbox 360 controller, or even an Xbox 360 replacement for the PS3, but if you're a gamer who prefers the Xbox 360 controller layout, then this is definitely worth a look.  Here's my side by side observations of the Pro Elite vs the Dualshock vs the Xbox 360 controller:  LOOK & FEEL:  First to note is that the Pro Elite is larger than the Dualshock controller.  It is approximately the same size and shape as an Xbox 360 controller, but it is lighter in weight than the Xbox controller (about the same weight of a Dualshock), has textured handle grips, and orange glowing buttons.  I don't mind the grip except to say that it's a little rough on your hands after you've gripped it tightly for a few hours.  However, I find the Xbox-inspired ergonomic styling of the body easier and more comfortable to hold than the Dualshock controller.  The Pro Elite's thumb stick placement is more in keeping with the Xbox controller rather than the Dualshock.  All the buttons glow orange and have the corresponding PS3 symbol on them.  If you are used to the Dualshock button placement, you won't have any trouble with them not being color-coded.  The thumb sticks are about as 'firm' as the Dualshock, which is to say, a little softer than the Xbox controller.  I prefer the Xbox-style placement of these thumb sticks over that of the Dualshock.  I have not experienced any 'dead zone' -- I am not saying it's not there, as other reviewers have noted, just that I have not experienced it.  I mostly play games like \\"Uncharted\\" and \\"Infamous\\" and some puzzle games.  The triggers are still Dualshock-style (ie: recessed backwards), but I added some [[ASIN:B001AW156U Pelican PS3 Dual L / R Triggers Controller Attachments for Playstation 3]] to them, which helps jut them forward.  The dual rumble is very nice, and seems comparable with the Dualshock in intensity.  SETUP:  The Pro Elite Wireless Controller requires a receiver dongle to be plugged into the front of your PS3, and paired with the controller (synced) to use.  Once synced, it as a good wireless range for any room.  Although this version of the Pro Elite is wireless, it charges through this dongle via a cable, and while charging, I found the cord way too short to be able to charge and play comfortably.  Also, the dongle must remain plugged into the front of your PS3 at all times (when using the Pro Elite).  CONCLUSION:  Being a gamer who prefers the Xbox-style controller, I could not be happier with the Pro Elite Wireless Controller for my PS3.  It suits my play style and I'm glad I found it.

0553208845|4.0|Siddhartha is one of the most famous novels of all time. It follows the physical and spiritual life a man from his youth into adulthood. While Siddhartha does have a narrative arc, it feels generous to call this a novel, not because of its length, but because it is more or less a vehicle for Herman Hesse to pass down his particular philosophy. That's not to say Siddhartha is bad, in fact my translation by Hilda Rosner, is particularly beautiful. Also, the three main characters of the novel are fascinating and, despite what others might say, are, to me, three dimensional.<br /><br />It is said that Siddhartha parallels that of the Buddha himself, and from what I know that's true enough. But I think this book is worthy of another comparison: Ecclessiastes. Both contain wisdom from a man who has experienced everything. The major difference is that, while Ecclesiastes, minus the last few verses, by in large interprets life pessimistically, Siddhartha interprets life in an eternal optimism.<br /><br />Strong critics of eastern philosophy won't have their minds changed by this one. At the same time, I think it is impossible to call Siddhartha anything but life-affirming. It is clear that Hesse had a very profound way of looking at the world.

1451627289|5.0|OK, I'm a Stephen King fan, though I have read only half of his work.  I have had misgivings about the excessive length of much of his fiction, supposing that his record as a best-selling author has kept editors from tightening novels that, with a different author's name on them, might have been shortened considerably.  But that's not entirely fair, since much of the length of his books comes from the care he takes to make his characters \\"real\\" to his readers, and this is as much a Stephen King trademark as his often macabre themes.<br /><br />Although \\"11/22/63\\" is one of his longest yet, for me it was one of the most satisfying.  First, it was a superior foray into the time travel genre, which has always appealed to me.  Second, Mr. King's evident nostalgia for the period in question overlaps greatly with my own, being of similar ages.  And finally, although there are good guys and bad guys aplenty, this is not a novel in which a supernatural and evil force is the enemy.  Rather, our hero Jake Epping/George Amberson is up against a past that simply resists change.  As he repeatedly says, \\"The past is obdurate.\\"<br /><br />(Minor Spoilers Follow)  The first, lengthy section of the book is devoted to Jake's efforts to prove whether the past can be changed, and is a book unto itself.  Having satisfied himself on that point, he then embarks on an effort to pin down as much as possible whether Oswald was acting alone.  That is, if he wasn't alone, a quest to prevent JFK's assassination by stopping Oswald might be fruitless.  He then seeks to stop Oswald.  The final section seemed a bit rushed in comparison, but was satisfying nonetheless.  There is an attempt to provide an explanation of how time travel works and its consequences, but it was not the book's strongest part for me.<br /><br />In short, if you like time travel fiction, this is about as good as it gets, and if you like Stephen King's writing, this is Mr. King at the top of his game.

It really no longer is about the end of the world. It is about petty people who seem to have missed the memo about the world having come to an end and who, as a result, engage in these petty quarrels with one another as if nothing had changed.  This show was great because it looked at the whole picture: zombie apocalypse, the struggle to survive and character development, the science behind the disease, the grander questions of survival, traversing the tough road. Now it is just focusing on the personal dramas of a few characters who seem to live in their own bubbles. Disappointed is an understatement. They writers go on like this and this show will get cancelled mid season 3. It is a shame because it was so damn good in the beginning. 0143117009|4.0|Diamond uses a comparative study to uncover why some societies fail for environmental reasons while others succeed.  As such Diamond looks to case studies.  He uses a five-point framework to do so.<br /><br />Collapsed societies: Easter Island, Pitcairn Island & Henderson Island, the Anasazi in the American Southwest, the Mayans, and Norse Greenland.<br />Success stories: Tikopia, the New Guinea highlands, and the Japan of the Tokugawa Era.<br />Modern case studies: southwestern Montana, Rwanda, Dominican Republic, China, and Australia.<br />Five-point Framework: environmental damage, climate change, hostile neighbors, friendly trading partners, and the society's response to its environmental problems.<br /><br />Diamond believes that these collapses were not inevitable.  Nor are they the mere province of less \\"advanced\\" societies.  Key to establishing this are the three chapters on Norse Greenland, a medieval European society that failed while the Intuit society occupying the same landmass survived.  Diamond posits persuasively it was because of the choices made by the individual societies.<br /><br />Diamond has no sacred cows.  He's willing to explode the myth of indigenous people living in concert with nature.  Nor is he willing to give business (err, \\"big business\\") a pass.  They gets a fair shake.  Diamond finds that Big Oil, that old bugaboo, isn't a very big polluter these days.  Far, far more problematic is surface mining.<br /><br />This is economic destruction on the micro, not macro, scale.  The public conversation on environmental issues has become dominated by global warming.  Deforestation and similar issues can still catastrophically wreck an environment on their own.<br /><br />Collapse is a dry read.  The format largely forces this, although it also allows Diamond to lay out his case in a systematic manner.

Unfortunately, the main plot doesn't in my mind live up to the promise of the first part of the book.  <Minor Spoilers Ahead - I won't give away important plot points, but I will discuss the general direction of the book to express my issues.>  I was interested when the main plot line started up about 1/4 the way in to see where it would go.  At first, the mystery was intriguing.  However, as more and more of the main plot was revealed, the more disappointed I became. The hero doesn't really ever outsmart anyone in a notable way except one character to get a new set of clothes (one of the better sections of the later part of the book). If you want an interesting and unique fantasy world with a hard to predict direction, you may enjoy this book, but if you are looking for a well orchestrated caper where the main character outclasses his enemies and leaves you appreciative of the subtleties (as the first part of the book indicated) I can't recommend it.

B003W5C0VW|5.0|There are two kinds of Rolling Stones fans.  The first kind was alive and rocking when the band delivered the one-two gut punch of [[ASIN:B001WCN23W Sticky Fingers]] and [[ASIN:B0039TD7RC Exile on Main Street]] (1971 and 1972, respectively). The Beatles had called it a day...no more yearly \\"events\\" like [[ASIN:B0025KVLTM Sgt. Pepper's Lonely Hearts Club Band (Remastered)]] and [[ASIN:B0025KVLUQ Abbey Road (Remastered)]]. If the Stones were in fact \\"the greatest rock & roll band in the world,\\" as is proclaimed on the opening of [[ASIN:B00006AW2K Get Yer Ya-Ya's Out!]], the playing field was a lot less crowded in 71-72. Their major competition came in the form of The Who ([[ASIN:B00008LOS1 Who's Next (Deluxe Edition)]] in 1971 and [[ASIN:B005DMNS4A Quadrophenia]] in 1973). But in terms of straight-ahead, Chuck Berry-style, riff-driven, testosterone-driven rock and roll, The Stones WERE the greatest, especially during Mick Taylor's tenure. In order to be the \\"first kind of\\" Stones fan, you had to be there as it unfolded. You had to walk into your local \\"record Store\\" and peel the cellophane off of your copy of \\"Sticky Fingers\\" and \\"Exile\\" and place it on your turntable and lower the tonearm and hear the crackle of needle hitting vinyl and hear the opening \\"fire in the hole\\" blasts of \\"Sticky Fingers\\" or \\"Rocks Off\\" for the FIRST time. I was / am \\"that kind of Stones fan\\"...I was there.  The second kind heard the music later, perhapes decades later, and may have discovered it through the many bands who were formed and influenced by being \\"the first kind of\\" Stones fan. You've probably heard Roger (The Byrds) McGuinn's anecdote about seeing The Beatles' [[ASIN:B004U7MQXS A Hard Day's Night]] and immediately deciding that he needed to form a band. He wasn't alone. There were many, back in the day, who heard Mick Taylor and Keith Richards send out salvo after salvo of deadly blues and R&B flavored licks while Jagger prowled the stage and Watts and Wyman soldiered on, expressionless, and thought \\"THIS is my sandbox. THIS is where I need to be playing.\\"  This DVD, despite any technical imperfections of the era, captures the Sones when they were at their undeniable peak. In the accompanying booklet, Jagger mentions the fact that the Stones could have off nights and be sloppy:  \\"It's a full-on performance and energy levels are really high...Everyone's very together and on, you know. I can remember the Rolling Stones being, sometimes, very lackadaisical. a very sloppy band on stage, at this period. But that was obviously not the case on this day.\\"  This was the Exile On Main Street tour. Just as Ron Wood was essentially a \\"guest\\" on [[ASIN:B001WCN23M Black and Blue]] and went full throttle on [[ASIN:B005N95JA4 Some Girls: Deluxe Edition]], Taylor did the same on \\"Sticky Fingers.\\"  It's a sheer joy watching Jagger do his best rooster strut as Keef rips into \\"Happy\\" like a pit bull tearing into a pork chop. Jagger takes his time with the \\"tease\\" section of \\"Midnight Rambler\\" (\\"Oh NO, baby...you can't DO that, baby...), before blood-red stage lights bathe him in his white jumpsuit howling \\"Waaaal, you HEARD about the BAW-stunnn...\\" Taylor and Richards lock horns behind him, spewing out raw, sustained blues licks while Wyman and Watts hold the bottom firm and the entire band erupts in lock-step precision.  THIS is rock and roll. The hype about the Rolling Stones? There IS no hype, just the music. The same as the Beatles. It wasn't great because your mom and dad liked it. It was great because it was great, and it still is. For decades later, this music on this DVD will show you no mercy.  A true gem if there ever was one.

0517548232|5.0|I purchased the 1979 edition of Hazlitt's book as a MBA candidate at Pepperdine University.  My professor, the erudite and sharp minded Dr. George Reisman, wanted to instill in his students the fundamental principle of liberty and how government attempted to disrupt and eradicate that liberty at every opportunity.  Intervention was its means and totalitarianism was its method.  Hazlitt is no exception when it comes to upholding liberty.  His book attests to the vitality of his work. The book's rankings on Amazon demonstrates how oppressed people seek for answers to fend off the oppressors and their interventionists policies through a corrupt government. He begins the work at the place Ken Starr did in a lecture I attended in which he reviewed his prosecutorial experience with former President Clinton: the broken window. There are striking parallels between the economic principle and the moral principle that Starr applied.  Both involved the impact of vandelism in a corrupt society.  The two vandels existed in separate segments of society, but the net effect was the same: the destruction of long term value that may not be visible to the naked eye.  One was theft of private property through its destruction (the broken window) and the other the theft of societal morals through political corruption.  One might ask, \\"How in the world can you make this parallel?\\"  Easy.  Hazlitt argues from the fallacy of destruction.  When destruction occurs in any area of society, whether in economics or politics, part of society gets destroyed.  He argues that economists who argue for the destructiveness of war see it as a good thing when the opposite is true.  When destruction occurs, it wipes out capital, and when that happens, wealth declines.  The same is true in Clinton's case, except the capital of a moral society declined (and it continues to do so thanks to an immoral president).  Adam Smith makes it clear that economics and morality work hand in hand.  Hazlitt confirms this.The other part of destruction is when the government intervenes through heavy taxation and regulation.  It performs a similar theft of capital by converting capital into consumption (i.e., public works projects) and stifles the growth of capital, productivity, and the economy.  Hazlitt wrote his first edition just after the destructive reign of Roosevelt who presided over the greatest destructive measures in US history with his heavy taxation, tarriffs, deficit spending, and regulations.  We saw the longest capital destroying Depression in modern history followed by one of the most destructive wars this nations every experienced that sucked capital out of the economy to feed the war through deficit financing.  Hazlitt covers both of these types of destruction to the economy successfully.  While it appears that war creates capital, this is deceitful.  All war does is shift capital from other industries toward the industries that support the war.  A further aggrevation of the economy was rationing.  With the shifting of capital toward consumption and destruction, rationing of scarce supplies occurred, and guess who was in charge of it?  That's right!  The government.  The result of rationing industries from where capital got shifted to support war industries had their capital stolen, thereby leading to rationing of what was left over in those industries.  As these war industries absorbed capital for producing war equipment that gets destroyed, there is insufficient capital reinvested.  Conseqently, the entire economy suffered.  Then Hazlitt gets into a topic that burns hot today - picking winners and losers among industries and companies.  Hazlitt observed the same problem then as exists now with the Obama administration: proping up industries (banks, auto, etc.) at the expense of taxpayers (both individuals and companies).  The results is corporate cronyism and corruption.  This is how economics and morality get married.  In essense, Obama is no different from Clinton in destroying the moral fiber of our nation through the reallocation of wealth and its destruction while he does it.  Hazlitt saw that almost 80 years ago and warned us about it.  Those who argue against Hazlitt and his economics are really short-sighted in not being able to see all sides of the economic picture.  They miss the point of the very simple but powerful illustration Hazlitt provides at the beginning about the broken window.  They see only the consumption side (the loss and new replacement that makes it appear that nothing happened) and not the capital side (the siphoning off of capital through destruction so that eventual capital is lost).  Hazlitt points out this ignorance, which makes his message long lasting and popular.  Free people do not like to be controlled whether by government, unions (which Hazlitt also addresses), or vandels.  All three of these entities not only destroy or reduce capital but also the moral principles behind the creation of capital.  They produce nothing because that is not their purpose.  Their purpose is destruction and control, the opposite of liberty, moral sentiments (Adam Smith), and productivity that leads to a vibrant economy.

As far as I could tell, the only way I could design a repeating pattern would be to wrap a piece of paper around a tube and draw on it. That sounded borderline ridiculous so I needed this book.  Kim's book satisfied my curiosity, (no tubes!) but it did so much more as well.  First of all, a week after I read it I ended up designing my first fabric. Coincidence? Absolutely not. This book gave me the lift I needed to go from a good idea to an actual design. It helped me understand some important concepts that I could apply to the tools I had available. In my case, an iPad with a simple illustration program, but whatever your supplies are, even if they are just pencil and paper, there are instructions for you too. Do you want to do screen printing or block printing? Do you want to design with Photoshop or Illustrator for digital printing? All the steps are explained for whichever way you'd like to design.  The book also prepared me for dealing with color variation between my computer screen and online printers. Information on printing processes, types of fabric, and color theory are in there as well. There is a lot in the book I didn't expect. Some of it may not be useful to me right now (for instance, things to consider if licensing your designs) but I have to say I'm impressed at how thorough the book is. I can see that whether I just want to create an occasional fabric design, or if I get bitten by the bug and decide to pursue it as a serious venture, I will refer to A Field Guide to Fabric Design over and over in the coming years. 0307352145|4.0|\\"Shut Up!\\" has become a mall moll's colloquialism to express disbelief.  The watchword cautions a noisy crowd into meditative reception.  It is a catchphrase that lessens the loud babbler and empowers the silent observer.  In other words, as the book's title suggests, we should stop our palaver and ponder the quiet person.  Susan Cain executes a fine defense of moderate people in a frenetic world.  A former Wall Street attorney, she has presented an academic product as is evidenced by approximately 47 pages of endnotes as well as her 16-page introduction.  Citing clinical studies, expert opinions, and professional comments, she traces the history of her subject from individual chivalry and personal character to our current culture of marketable personality.  Her exemplars and case studies substantiate the biological differences as well as some cultural nuances surrounding our understanding of extroverts and introverts.  And yet, no person is completely outgoing or entirely introspective.  There is balance, and we may have elements of both behavioral traits.  Cain's compilation of material and weight of argument guides us to nod in agreement that we live in a society that is fixated on outgoing stylists rather than the ponderous sustainers.  We live in a world of yin-and-yang; there is no extrovert without an introvert present.  Her elucidation might prompt a private understanding of our personality makeup.  A final chapter offers suggestions to parents and teachers about cultivating the introverted child.  But Cain's creation is more a textbook than a self-help guide.  Although her style is readable, it tends to be treatise-like and rather dry, lacking the hype, glibness, or glossy banter of a self-help guru akin to John Gray's \\"Men are from Mars...\\" franchise.  While comprehending this book you might experience some self-awareness--an \\"aha\\" moment--that cries out: \\"I'm not an introvert; I'm merely highly sensitive.\\"  But you'll not discover that epiphany unless youhush up and read this book.

B005KDWUFA|5.0|First things first - this album is different. From even the coverart, you can tell that Il Divo have this time gone for lighter shades in how they present themselves and how they sing, without having that overt darkness to their appearance and music. Which means, if you are expecting another song like 'Adagio' from the previous album, you'll be disappointed. Only a very small bit of this album goes up into the high drama stakes that the likes of Adagio from the last album set themselves up for.  The title track is clearly the standout... rather than take an already powerfully sung song like they have before, they've turned a once soft song into a huge burst of sound. In other words - they've completely re-interpretted it. And on this song you'll realise that the harmonies this time around have improved, by mixing voices differently. Different voices paired up at the right time is what really carries this song, and with that, the whole record.  IN A NUTSHELL - the harmonies are more varied on Wicked Game and LESS PREDICTABLE as they have been on the previous albums. While the song choices on 'The Promise' were a bit of a stinker, 'Siempre' had the least interesting harmonies down on the table for the men of Il Divo to sing.  Crying/Llorando: very poignant and breathtaking. I don't like the slight echo effect they've added to the start of the song, it highlights the range of each of them well. It also has Carlos singing very beautifully VERY SOFTLY for a change, which I personally love to hear.  Don't Cry For Me Argentina: yet again, Carlos brings out the best of his baritone voice, without just trying to sound like/complete with the tenors. This song really shows off how well Seb's voice has matured and grown.  Dove' L'Amore: adaption of Samuel Barber's Adagio. This shows off Seb's new-found power in his voice, as well as how well Urs can roll his words around in his mouth. The problem with this song is more than half of the song is sung at full-pelt. It becomes a bit too full on for me.  Falling Slowly/Te Prometo: An absolutely beautiful song. I often complain that the likes of Bocelli and othe crossover artists like Blake and The Canadian Tenors put just as much effort into the compositions of the music as they do with their singing, whereas Il Divo usually do not. This song HAS THAT BEAUTIFUL MUSIC that you probably crave if you are like me... the soft strings go very well with all of their voices in this song. It's one of the only true ballads of the album. It ends very suddenly, but yet again, i think Il DIvo is doing that just to surprise us in case we were expecting 'just the same as usual.'  Come What May/Te Amare: this song mirrors the classics of Il Divo. It's like the 'My Way' of this album, with a whole bunch of high-C's added to the end to showcase what David is really capable of. It has a surprisingly long intro, but the music is exquisite.  Senza Parole: Very, very dramatic song. It's only one of two originals on the CD, and is in Italian. Again the quality of the music has been stepped up a huge notch - you would not have heard such well composed music on anything since their first album so far. There is actually a male choir singing behind them in the first and last choruses for added dramatic effect.  Stay/Ven A Mi: In a bit of a twist, the music played at the very start of this song is keyboard. Yet again, they are goimg for something different so you're not listening to something that sounds like a song you've heard before. It's very pretty and it's magic comes from being very uncomplicated, which is probably why it reminds me a lot of their original song 'Rejoice' from the Christmas album.  Sempre Sempre: Their other original song, also in Italian. It's also very dramatic (they probably sound too much alike, unfortunately) however this has a very satisfying \\"boom boom boom boom!\\" on the drums at the end of the song.  Time To Say Goodbye: Carlos singing the start of this song reminds me of why I prefer to hear baritone versions of traditionally tenor songs, because there is a bit more warmth to the sound, so it sounds better than most versions to me. Urs reminds us that he is still the best at Italian on the second verse, and David gets his chance to hit those G's he loves so much on the first chorus. Great way to end a great album, however this song is dominated a bit too much by Carlos.  The only negative thing here is that yet again, they have released an album starved of original songs, and the original songs that they have put on this album won't stand the test of time like the originals of past albums.

B004H1V5RU|5.0|This game is fantabulous (that's a technical term).  I got it on Jan. 20, 2012 from my brother as a gift, and it has taken me a month of around an hour every few days to be able to go from start to finish in one sitting, which I just did a few minutes ago.  My brother's kids have the two easier versions and he doesn't own this one, but figured it would be a good thing for me, and he was right.  For those who don't know, the Perplexus Epic is a clear, hard plastic sphere about 8 inches in diameter, inside of which is a small steel ball (ball bearing size) and a maze of tracks.  There are 125 components to the track and the idea is to navigate the ball from the starting platform (1) to the ending \\"bucket\\" (125).  In a month of usage, I haven't had any issues with the quality.  I haven't dropped it, and am otherwise careful not to hurl it across the room in frustration (what I'm saying is that this game is probably not for those prone to physical violence).  The inside of my sphere has no loose pieces and there are no obstructions to the track (evidently has been an issue with some earlier versions).  This is definitely a nifty piece of game engineering.  Most of the track components are clearly numbered.  The track is hugely varied and some parts are trivial (such as a track with side walls) while others take different types of dexterity.  With some I make delicate movements (59, 98), others want the ball to have or acquire momentum (44->45, 77->78), with some I make quick movements (3, 99, 123), and with some I hop the ball (eg. on stairs), and a there are a few aerials.  With some, the trick is to figure out what the trick is - there were a few sections of track where it took me a LONG time to figure out a trivial way to do it because it looked like the track was asking for the impossible (eg. 20->21, 43->44->45, 64->65, 90->91, 111->112).  For some of the other ones, what was impossible at the beginning is now easy since I've become used to it.  One reviewer said that many of the steps are a matter of luck, but I think that most of them are a matter of skill once you've figured out the technique.  There are a few, though (49->50, 77->78, 79->80, 123->124) that I still find dicey and am sure I'm doing poorly.  While many of the track components are very easy, from personal experience I can say it is possible to jump the ball out of the track on almost all of them.  I like the fact that you can enter the ball onto the track at components 33, 56, and 91.  In addition, by jumping the ball it's possible to take shortcuts to a few areas of practise.  For example, jumping the ball from 92 to 122 would allow one to practice either 123 or (after a drop ontp 77) 77->78.

0062119044|3.0|\\"We Need to Talk About Kevin\\" is a fictional story of a mother who is desperate to figure out why her 15 year old son would carry out his plan to murder in cold blood seven classmates, an English teacher, and a cafeteria worker while on school grounds.  Evoking memories of Erik Harris and Dyland Klebold, Kip Kinkel, and other youth who bring bloodshed to school, this book tries to show the life of how one teenager may make this fatal decision and perhaps the signs that should have been seen leading up to it.  I'm not sure if the author was trying to point out that there are always signs, but often parents don't want to see them.  It definitely felt that this was what she was trying to do with the child in this story, Kevin Khatchadourian.  It also helped to illustrate what happens to the family that is left to deal with the aftermath.  The story is told in letters that the mother, Eva Khatchadourian, writes to her husband who is no longer in the picture.  It isn't clear until the end why he is not there.  Did he blame her for what happened?  The book is written for the general reader who is interested in trying to understand what makes schoolyard killers to, first, desire to kill, and second, to actually follow through on it.  Were their signs that should have been seen while the child was young that the parents missed?  Were they born this way?  Nature v. Nurture is definitely a running theme.  To be honest, I found the first half of the book tiresome to get through and also stopped reading.  It seemed like it was mostly about a \\"woe is me\\" mother and how life has been unfair about her.  I wasn't interested in how unfortunate she felt in how her life turned out, but how her son came to be who he was.  Luckily, the story picks up about half-way and becomes less about the mother and more about Kevin and also about Kevin's father and sister.  The second half was hard to put down and I was glad that I didn't give up on the book.  \\"We Need to Talk About Kevin\\" is a bookwhose subject matter is apt to get most people's attention.  We all desire to get into the minds of people who do acts that are so unthinkable so that we can understand why they did what they did.  Could they have been stopped?  Were they just misunderstood?  We never really find out in this book since it is told from the viewpoint of the mother.  Seeing how she had to deal with the consequences was definitely interesting.  She struggles to figure out what could have been done differently and will probably never find an answer to that.  As a reader, I would love to see a companion book that tells this story from Kevin's point of view.  Overall this was an interesting read and the second half of the book really picks up, whereas I felt the first half was hard to get through.  I am glad that I stuck with it because it did come to the point where I wasn't able to put down the book until its conclusion.

B003XF1XOQ|4.0|I decided to write this review to help skew the plethora of bad reviews that have been posted for Mockingjay.  While I understand and empathize with the negative reviews, no book is ever going to please everyone.  The series ended exactly as it should have, while staying true to its characters and the central plot.  No, it wasn't a bliss-filled ending, and yes there were points that were glossed-over perhaps a little too flippantly, but overall Mockingjay is every bit as good as its predecessors.  What I like about Suzanne Collins is that she tells a very real, very human story in each of the Hunger Games books.  Mockingjay is no exception, and even goes so far as to complicate our feelings about Katniss, who is a complex and flawed heroine in many ways.  There are times in Mockingjay where you find yourself thinking she is overly cold, calculating, and vengeful.  And then there are times when you realize she is merely a product of the Capitol's infinite cruelty.  What would you do?  I found myself cheering her at the end, for *SPOILER ALERT!* not being duped by the promises of District 13, or Coin's dubious agenda.  Her final vengeance was exactly as it should have been.  Collins took a bold stride in creating Katniss.  I predict her character, as well as this series, will be read and studied timelessly, alongside such masterpieces as Fahrenheit 451, 1984, and Brave New World.  The only reason I didn't give this book 5 stars is that I felt parts of the ending were wrapped up a little too neatly.  Don't read on if you don't like spoilers.  When Finnick is killed by the reptilian mutts, it reads like an afterthought.  Indeed, like Katniss, we have no time to consider the loss of his character, nor to mourn him.  Secondly, even as a man I was taken aback by the way Katniss surrenders to her fate with Peeta.  She never really chooses between him and Gale, she simply accepts that because he shows up to join her in District 12, that she must settle for him.  This  struckme as a very anti-feminist sentiment.  As the person holding the choice in her hands, Katniss should have asserted her preference, not the other way around.  Of course we were all rooting for Peeta, as the author intended us to do, but after everything she experienced, Katniss ends up accepting the decision handed to her, a glaring side-step away from her character's strength and independence.  Overall Suzanne Collins has left the world a masterpiece of allegorical fiction, and I highly recommend that anyone who has started this series not be shied away by the poor reviews.  It is 100% worth reading, and minor disappointments or not, you will be glad you read it.

I don't typically read mystery/thrillers, but I guess I don't necessarily place this book in the typical thriller category with Stephen King or James Patterson.  Rosamund Lupton was a new author to me since SISTER is her first novel.  Again, I am thrilled to find a new author that I can follow and someone whose first novel I really liked.<br /><br />Right away, you know that the character's sister has died, but you don't know how and the novel takes you through many twists and turns all the way to the absolute last page of the story.  It had me literally sitting on the edge of my seat as I was reading through the last 2 chapters.<br /><br />The story is told to me the reader as if I am the dead sister.  So, it took some getting used to as the living sister (Beatrice) is recounting the events of her sister's (Tess's) death through the telling of a letter as well as through the telling of the events to a lawyer.  There are divisions in the book so you know when there is a change in the telling of the story, but at times it was still confusing.<br /><br />The story is set in London. Since I have never been to London I am not familiar with the places the writer talked about, but her descriptions allowed me to easily picture the parks, the weather, and the scenes in the story.  I also appreciated that I was reading it on the Kindle and could easily find the definitions of the British terms that were used.<br /><br />Another great perk to reading on the Kindle is being able to highlight certain passages along the way. B000JMKQX0|5.0|World War Z by Max Brooks was a great retrospective novel about the near destruction of the human race by zombies.  The book is written a few years after the war officially ends, each chapter told by a different person, getting a very wide array of perspectives on the war.  I loved how the whole story was presented as a factual government report, after reading the first 50 pages, the novel read so logically that I nearly thought this event had actually taken place.  I think that Brooks captured the essence of each character that is interviewed and presents them in a very realistic manner.  From the burned out soldier that has seen too much death to make it after the war, to the opportunistic corporate CEO still in hiding for marketing a fake cure, to the child whose parents tried to kill her to keep her from being turned into a Z, to the doctor who attempted to treat \\"patient zero\\", these tales stick with you and are told with such passion that it doesn't matter that they are fiction, you WANT to believe it.  This book is not the typical post apocalyptic zombie tale.  What this book lacks in action it makes up for in humanity, both the good and bad sides.  I couldn't find anything wrong with this book other than I wanted 200 more pages and more characters to hear from.

1577314808|4.0|A few summers back, a loved one gave me a copy of A New Earth [[ASIN:0452289963 A New Earth: Awakening to Your Life's Purpose (Oprah's Book Club, Selection 61)]] by Eckhart Tolle that she picked up at a church sale. That book was the sequel to The Power of Now, and to be honest, I preferred it to The Power of Now. That does not mean I didn't enjoy this book - just that I found A New Earth to be more worthwhile. The basic premise of The Power of Now is that we live in the past and in our hope for the future, and replay certain memories over and over, and that we should be fully committed to the Now. There is a lot of truth to this. Tolle tackles many subjects including Ego, The Origin of Fear etc. (from Page 73 - \\"Fear seems to have many causes. Fear of loss, fear of failure, fear of being hurt, and so on, but ultimately all fear is the ego's fear of death, of annihilation...For example, even such a seemingly trivial and \\"normal\\" thing as the compulsive need to be right in an argument and make the other person wrong - defending the mental position with which you have identified - is due to the fear of death. If you identify with a mental position, then if you are wrong, your mind-based sense of self is seriously threatened with annihilation.\\") For me, the book started picking up around Page 90 when Tolle wrote \\"For example, it is estimated that as many as 50 million people were murdered to further the cause of communism, to bring about a \\"better world\\" in Russia, China, and other countries. This is a chilling example of how belief in a future heaven creates a present hell.\\" On page 216 in a chapter entitled \\"Dissolving the Collective Female Pain-Body\\", Tolle writes \\"For example, certain races or countries in which extreme forms of strife and violence occur have a heavier collective pain-body than others. Anyone with a strong pain-body and not enough consciousness to dis-identify from it will not only continuously or periodically be forced to relive their emotional pain but may also easily become either the perpetrator or the victim of violence, depending on whether their pain-body is predominantly active or passive.\\" On Pages 241-242 in a chapter entitled Using And Relinquishing Negativity, Tolle writes \\"Once you have identified with some form of negativity, you do not want to let go, and on a deeply unconscious level, you do not want positive change. It would threaten your identity as a depressed, angry, or hard-done-by person. You will then ignore, deny or sabotage the positive in your life. This is a common phenomenon. It is also insane.\\" On page 265, Tolle provides good, healthy advice \\"Then look at the specifics of the situation. Ask yourself, \\"Is there anything I can do to change the situation, improve it, or remove myself from it?\\" If so, you take appropriate action. Focus not on the 100 things that you will or may have to do at some future time but on the one thing that you can do now.\\" I remember times in my life where I'd worked in psychologically \\"unhealthy\\" work situations, and wish I'd had this advice at that time. But then again, it is also a human trait to get a sort of \\"high\\" out of complaining about a life situation, enjoying telling people how miserable you are in a situation and not doing something risky such as leaving a job because you've talked yourself into thinking it's the best you can do at a given time. Tolle deals with these issues on page 267 when he writes \\"You say that you are conscious of your unhappy feelings, but the truth is that you are identified with them and keep the process alive through compulsive thinking. All that is unconscious.\\"  I remember reading a book by another author, I believe was Ted Andrews, and he wrote that you should take out what works for you from any book, and throw out what doesn't. There are things in the Power of Now that I don't agree with, but there is also plenty that is worthwhile. So my advice is to read this book and cherry-pick what works for you, what adviceyou find to be constructive. It is an interesting, but not always perfect tome. Enjoy.
